<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_dca93e35-e10e-492f-b327-9f26aa308066'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer9296439986067400158">This is the original cover edition of <b><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25630779.ISBN__0374515360' title='ISBN:	0374515360' rel='nofollow noopener'>ISBN:	0374515360</a> (ISBN13: 9780374515362</b><br/><br/><b>Winner of the National Book Award</b><br/><br/>The publication of this extraordinary volume firmly established Flannery O'Connor's monumental contribution to American fiction. There are thirty-one stories here in all, including twelve that do not appear in the only two story collections O'Connor put together in</span>
  <span id="freeText9296439986067400158" style="display:none">This is the original cover edition of <b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25630779.ISBN__0374515360" title="ISBN:&#9;0374515360" rel="nofollow noopener">ISBN:	0374515360</a> (ISBN13: 9780374515362</b><br /><br /><b>Winner of the National Book Award</b><br /><br />The publication of this extraordinary volume firmly established Flannery O'Connor's monumental contribution to American fiction. There are thirty-one stories here in all, including twelve that do not appear in the only two story collections O'Connor put together in her short lifetime - <i>Everything That Rises Must Converge </i>and <i>A Good Man Is Hard to Find</i>. <br /><br />O'Connor published her first story, "The Geranium," in 1946, while she was working on her master's degree at the University of Iowa. Arranged chronologically, this collection shows that her last story, "Judgement Day" - sent to her publisher shortly before her death - is a brilliantly rewritten and transfigured version of "The Geranium." Taken together, these stories reveal a lively, penetrating talent that has given us some of the most powerful and disturbing fiction of the twentieth century. Also included is an introduction by O'Connor's longtime editor and friend, Robert Giroux.<br /><br />Contents:<br />The geranium -- <br />The barber -- <br />Wildcat -- <br />The crop -- <br />The turkey -- <br />The train -- <br />The peeler -- <br />The heart of the park -- <br />A stoke of good fortune -- <br />Enoch and the gorilla -- <br />A good man is hard to find -- <br />A late encounter with the enemy -- <br />The life you save may be your own -- <br />The river -- <br />A circle in the fire -- <br />The displaced person -- <br />A temple of the Holy Ghost -- <br />The artificial nigger -- <br />Good country people -- <br />You can't be any poorer than dead -- <br />Greenleaf -- <br />A view of the woods -- <br />The enduring chill -- <br />The comforts of home -- <br />Everything that rises must converge -- <br />The partridge festival -- <br />The lame shall enter first -- <br />Why do the heathen rage? -- <br />Revelation -- <br />Parker's back -- <br />Judgement Day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9296439986067400158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=284996 >

    <div class="u-positionRelative">

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/544392-too-bad-we-can-t-ask-but-i-do-know-that">Too bad we can&#39;t ask. But I do know that once someone asked Flannery O&#39;Conner &quot;Why do you write?&quot; Her answer, &quot;Because I&#39;m good at it.&quot; Another time at a reading she did, a woman in the audience said, &quot;That leaves a bad taste in my mouth,&quot; and O&#39;Conner answered, &quot;You were suppose to read it, not eat it.&quot;
My fav short story is &quot;Whatever Rises Must Converge.&quot;</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_544392'><a id="like_count_community_question_544392" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/544392?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">17 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F284996.The_Complete_Stories">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/544392-too-bad-we-can-t-ask-but-i-do-know-that">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/544392-too-bad-we-can-t-ask-but-i-do-know-that">Add your answer</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/36253202-rui"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1583227239i/36253202._UX100_CR0,16,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/36253202-rui">Rui</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It's not really a question, but thank you for sharing these quotes. I think they are quite representative of the introverted perfectionist O'Conner wa<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It's not really a question, but thank you for sharing these quotes. I think they are quite representative of the introverted perfectionist O'Conner was. She did not write to please the reader, or to gain recognition (like many of the characters in her stories). She dedicated her life perfecting little by little the art of translating human nature onto paper. To me, each story feels like a punch in the stomach, and reading through this complete collection feels like getting punched repeatedly. But somehow I'm loving it, and I want to keep reading.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/656332-was-she-a-racist-or-did-she-write-about">Was she a racist, or did she write about racists and racist society? How much was she a part of the culture and how much was she a reporter of it?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_656332'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F284996.The_Complete_Stories">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/656332-was-she-a-racist-or-did-she-write-about">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/656332-was-she-a-racist-or-did-she-write-about">See all 4 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/22709697-chris"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1445003627i/22709697._UY100_CR24,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/22709697-chris">Chris</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No, not racist (unless you think holding to some racial stereotypes racist). She wrote about society in general, from the south where she was from, in<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No, not racist (unless you think holding to some racial stereotypes racist). She wrote about society in general, from the south where she was from, in a very raw way. She was also writing at a time when the civil-rights movement of the 60s was in its infancy.<br /><br />I think she let her readers try to figure it out for the most part. Many of her stories have these ironies rooted in them that you have to think about. She doesn't just come out and say something should be wrong or right. But if you read some of these a few times and let them fester, I think you'll find she's saying a lot.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=recommend">recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3597505033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17277576662227816079">i have decided to become a genius.<br/><br/>to accomplish this, i'm going to work my way through the complete stories of various authors, reading + reviewing 1 story every day until i get bored / lose every single follower / am struck down by a vengeful deity.<br/><br/><b>PROJECT 1: THE COMPLETE STORIES BY FLANNERY O'CONNOR</b><br/><br/><u>DAY 1: THE GERANIUM</u><br/>it must be a real nightmare to be an old racist white guy. imagine what an unpleasant experience having that brain would be.<br/>good story though. go off, flannery. <br/>(anyone also who c</span>
  <span id="freeText17277576662227816079" style="display:none">i have decided to become a genius.<br /><br />to accomplish this, i'm going to work my way through the complete stories of various authors, reading + reviewing 1 story every day until i get bored / lose every single follower / am struck down by a vengeful deity.<br /><br /><b>PROJECT 1: THE COMPLETE STORIES BY FLANNERY O'CONNOR</b><br /><br /><u>DAY 1: THE GERANIUM</u><br />it must be a real nightmare to be an old racist white guy. imagine what an unpleasant experience having that brain would be.<br />good story though. go off, flannery. <br />(anyone also who can't separate the narrator from the author - by page 1 it's clear this is not the book for you.)<br /><i>rating: 3.5ish</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 2: THE BARBER</u><br />in a way it's reassuring to know that elections have been this horrible for decades.<br /><i>rating: 3</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 3: WILDCAT</u><br />well, i skipped two days, because saturday was my birthday and sunday i was quite busy nursing the hangover of a lifetime, but we're back in business.<br />this was not my favorite. also if every story contains this many n-words we might be going back out of business.<br /><i>rating: 2</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 4: THE CROP</u><br />this one just simply rules. loved it from the first sentence. can't even explain it, but i love Willie and i would die for her.<br /><i>rating: 4.5 stars</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 5: THE TURKEY</u><br />in my head this kid is played by Macaulay Culkin.<br />also, it takes a f*cking good writer to make you feel a tension and unease about a kid walking down the street with a turkey. go off, flannery.<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 6: THE TRAIN</u><br />i have once again forgotten i was doing this.<br />i'm going to be honest, this story did not do it for me. i saw what it was trying to do, the writing was good, i had moments of sympathy, but the overall conflict / backstory just didn't hit.<br /><i>rating: 2.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 7: THE PEELER</u><br />this is one where i want to spend the day thinking about it and let it sink in a little before i decide.<br />also, ain't religion confusing.<br /><i>rating: 2</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 8: THE HEART OF THE CITY</u><br />yesterday i said i wanted to think more about the story i read before rating it, and flannery o'connor complied by giving me another story about the same characters that allowed me to realize i hated both.<br /><i>rating: 2</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 9: A STROKE OF GOOD FORTUNE</u><br />i really liked this one. i don't want to say why i really liked it, because it spoils the whole thing, but i really liked it.<br /><i>rating: 4 or 4.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 10: ENOCH AND THE GORILLA</u><br />remember how earlier i was like, all confident that i was going to hate every story about this specific group of characters?<br />sorry flannery. i was wrong.<br />first time for everything, and all that.<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 11: A GOOD MAN IS HARD TO FIND</u><br />YESSSSSS.<br />this story made me miss being in college lit classes with such an intense, sharp nostalgia that it's probably going to be my cause of death.<br /><i>rating: 5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 12: A LATE ENCOUNTER WITH THE ENEMY</u><br />will i ever manage to get to this project on a saturday? no.<br />anyway the careful and complex feelings that flan gets you to have for each character in this story...masterful.<br /><i>rating: 4.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 13: THE LIFE YOU SAVE MAY BE YOUR OWN</u><br /><i>"The old woman's three mountains were black against the dark blue sky and were visited off and on by various planets and by the moon after it had left the chickens."</i><br />flannery please write my internal monologue.<br />great title too.<br /><i>rating: 3.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 14: THE RIVER</u><br />jesus christ, flannery!<br /><i>rating: 4.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 15: A CIRCLE IN THE FIRE</u><br />you really do start to get a sense of how these stories are going to go.<br /><i>rating: 3</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 16: THE DISPLACED PERSON</u><br />some super interesting race and xenophobia commentary in here, but also this was way too long for its purpose.<br /><i>rating: 3.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 17: A TEMPLE OF THE HOLY GHOST</u><br />i am liking the freak show motif for what it does, but not actually liking reading any of instances of it.<br />am liking the voice of this one though. also the title and what it means.<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 18: THE ARTIFICIAL N*****</u><br />i have to tell you i am really losing my ability to deal with these race exploration stories, teeming as they are with uses of the n-word.<br /><i>rating: 2</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 19: GOOD COUNTRY PEOPLE</u><br />what the hell and amazing, in equal parts.<br /><i>rating: 4.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 20: YOU CAN'T BE POORER THAN DEAD</u><br />for the first time in this whole reading experience, as soon as i started this i felt the urge to skip it. and i should have run with that feeling.<br /><i>rating: 2</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 21: GREENLEAF</u><br />they had me in the first half not going to lie!!!<br />(aka the first half of this did nothing for me but it won me over by the end.)<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 22: A VIEW OF THE WOODS</u><br />oh my god i love this. what is wrong with flannery o'connor and can every story have an ending like this one.<br /><i>rating: 4.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 23: THE ENDURING CHILL</u><br />reading 3 stories today because i missed 2 days and also i don't want to lug this tome home for thanksgiving. <br />this is like if flannery o'connor wrote a salinger story and therefore i love it more than anything.<br /><i>rating: 5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 24: THE COMFORTS OF HOME</u><br />i mean, like...okay?<br />the villain of this story is The Concept Of Getting Laid, so determine whether that choice does it for you before reading, i guess.<br /><i>rating: 3</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 25: EVERYTHING THAT RISES MUST CONVERGE</u><br />this would be more interesting if it weren't the 346728346738th deeply similar race story in this book. i like it more than some of the others, though.<br /><i>rating: 3.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 26: THE PARTRIDGE FESTIVAL</u><br />intellectuals do be silly, it's true.<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 27: THE LAME SHALL ENTER FIRST</u><br />not going to lie, this was way too long.<br /><i>rating: 3</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 28: WHY DO THE HEATHEN RAGE?</u><br />short and sweet.<br />well, not that sweet, but veryveryvery short.<br /><i>rating: 3</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 29: REVELATION</u><br />this was an interesting one. a thinker. hm.<br /><i>rating: 4</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 30: PARKER'S BACK</u><br />no thanks.<br /><i>rating: 2.5</i><br /><br /><u>DAY 31: JUDGEMENT DAY</u><br />at first i thought this was the same set of characters as The Geranium, but it wasn't, and then i thought maybe it was the same ones as in The Train, but it wasn't, and then overall i just felt like A Late Encounter with the Enemy should have been the closer instead of this one.<br /><i>rating: 2.5<br /><br /><u>OVERALL</u><br />these stories could feel a little bit repetitive reading them one after the other, but really flannery o'connor is a one of a kind, fantastic writer. 4 stars.</i></span>

<div id="review_205812919" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205812919" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6014887-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389632385p2/6014887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/205812919?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/6014887-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780374127527" href="/book/show/37487421-the-complete-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=southern-literature">southern-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=by-women">by-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=something-borrowed">something-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6014887-richard?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205812919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8499719029910525546">The stories in this collection were written by an unassuming yet serious Catholic woman from Georgia who, after devoting her short life to writing, died of lupus in 1964. Besides the stories, she had written two novels and started a third; one can only speculate what other masterpieces she would have written had she lived longer.<br/><br/>The stories are hard-bitten, bizarre and haunting. Two that I read years ago in college have stuck with me and are just as jarring today as they were then. O'Connor's th</span>
  <span id="freeText8499719029910525546" style="display:none">The stories in this collection were written by an unassuming yet serious Catholic woman from Georgia who, after devoting her short life to writing, died of lupus in 1964. Besides the stories, she had written two novels and started a third; one can only speculate what other masterpieces she would have written had she lived longer.<br /><br />The stories are hard-bitten, bizarre and haunting. Two that I read years ago in college have stuck with me and are just as jarring today as they were then. O'Connor's theme is the warpedness that resides deep in the human heart. Her protagonists are usually people who think quite highly of themselves. They are often nice people who are nice to everyone (within reasonable limits, of course) and think that the world would be a nicer place to live if only everyone were as nice as they are (“Good Country People”, “Everything that Rises Must Converge”). There are the people who dream nostalgically of a segregated society where inferiors knew their rank, respected their betters and did not try to move outside of their foreordained place in the pecking order (“The Geranium”, “Judgment Day”). Then there are the educated or artistic types who feel confined or bored by the life they lead, can't wait to escape, and sneer at all the inferior mortals around them (“Good Country People”, “The Enduring Chill”). And of course, there are those horrifying individuals who are evil, have surrendered themselves to it, and commit atrocities just because they can (“A Good Man is Hard to Find”, “The Lame Shall Enter First”).<br /><br />O'Connor's wrath and sarcasm are reserved most of all for those people who are so immured in themselves that they are unaware of their own blindness. Often there is a cataclysmic moment of epiphany when they are confronted at last with their own shortcomings. <br /><br />O'Connor lets these people show us their true colours either by enabling us to eavesdrop on their thoughts or by allowing us to listen to their conversations; she is a master of psychology and of dialogue. In her descriptions of the background scenery, nature often serves to highlight the desire of certain characters to escape their circumstances or the feeling of being trapped in an environment from which they cannot disentangle themselves without great effort. <br /><br />These stories are dark, bitter, angry and often tragic. But they are a brilliant barometer of the human heart and the depravity of which it is capable when left untouched by divine grace.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8499719029910525546" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2856606613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2856606613" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2856606613?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2856606613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1261910867839894922">ALL THE LONELY PEOPLE - <br/>WHERE DO THEY ALL COME FROM?<br/>ALL THE LONELY PEOPLE -<br/>WHERE DO THEY ALL BELONG?<br/>- Lennon & McCartney<br/><br/>GARLIC AND SAPPHIRE IN THE MUD<br/>CLOT THE BEDDED AXLE-TREE.<br/>- T.S. Eliot<br/><br/>Flannery O’Connor is a Wall. And she’s each Brick in that Wall - hard-edged; uncompromising; and made out of unyielding, obdurate Faith.<br/><br/>She’s not a “Nice” Writer.<br/><br/>Nor is she trying to be. Cause she’s trying to give us the Straight Goods.<br/><br/>No, she’s not living the Dream. She’s living the REALITY.<br/><br/>And yes, we all hav</span>
  <span id="freeText1261910867839894922" style="display:none">ALL THE LONELY PEOPLE - <br />WHERE DO THEY ALL COME FROM?<br />ALL THE LONELY PEOPLE -<br />WHERE DO THEY ALL BELONG?<br />- Lennon &amp; McCartney<br /><br />GARLIC AND SAPPHIRE IN THE MUD<br />CLOT THE BEDDED AXLE-TREE.<br />- T.S. Eliot<br /><br />Flannery O’Connor is a Wall. And she’s each Brick in that Wall - hard-edged; uncompromising; and made out of unyielding, obdurate Faith.<br /><br />She’s not a “Nice” Writer.<br /><br />Nor is she trying to be. Cause she’s trying to give us the Straight Goods.<br /><br />No, she’s not living the Dream. She’s living the REALITY.<br /><br />And yes, we all have plenty of goods in our lives - nice things to eat and drink in the fridge, nice gadgets to carry around with us everywhere, nice books, nice friends, and plenty of nice diversions.<br /><br />But life’s not nice, she says. And after life comes Death. And many, many people are Living their Deaths right here and now.<br /><br />She just gives us the facts.<br /><br />Especially about the countless moaning, mourning men and women who have fallen beneath the Iron Wheel of Karma RIGHT HERE AMONG US. And of the Countless Many who will follow.<br /><br />They fill our own inner cities. They fill our Third World. And their desperate emptiness also haunts the souls of our kids.<br /><br />Why?<br /><br />She never tells you up front, but if you know her background you’ll know why. Her stories scream the answer silently:<br /><br />We’ve forgotten God.<br /><br />And the Spirit has packed up and left our cities.<br /><br />And our Souls.<br /><br />Now, these are major, major personal allegations, but in her writing it’s all silently sous-entendu. So it won’t hurt you if you don’t want it to.<br /><br />For all she gives you are the facts about US. Cause no, it’s NOT about poor sharecroppers and inner city dwellers in the faraway Fifties... but you can form your own impressions.<br /><br />But even without such ‘antiquated’ views about perdition as she held, to me it’s about US. Us without all our diversions.<br /><br />So now - you don’t have to squirm under the intense pressure of all these perfect horrors, tightly packed and sealed into each miniature masterpiece of a messy, mundane tale depicting our grossly flawed lives.<br /><br />If you don’t want to hazard the risk. <br /><br />For the warning stands primarily for the sensitive, even though modern interpreters now call the viewpoint archaic and draconian anyway. Since it may confuse you, I leave that to your discretion.<br /><br />And, as well, she always mitigates it all with her clear-eyed objective lens. <br /><br />So you can judge her objectively according to your own worldview. And you’ll be safe!<br /><br />Or, if your POV is stoutly agnostic, and empathy is not a part of your personality, you’re in the clear too.<br /><br />But if you’re like me and you go with the flow of your books empathetically - cave libris!<br /><br />For new age Jeremiah that she is, she has served us notice.<br /><br />Wake up to Life or fall Beneath the Wheel!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1261910867839894922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2069402337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2069402337" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2069402337?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374515362" href="/book/show/25630779-the-complete-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=national-book-award">national-book-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=1001-before-you-die">1001-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=southern-gothic">southern-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=american">american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2069402337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15117958360863813157">I feel like I've just been to school. (That's a good thing.) I read each of these 31 stories - a compilation of both <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48464.A_Good_Man_is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories' title='A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories by Flannery O'Connor' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218659.Everything_That_Rises_Must_Converge_Stories' title='Everything That Rises Must Converge Stories by Flannery O'Connor' rel='nofollow noopener'>Everything That Rises Must Converge: Stories</a>, as well as 12 other stories, 6 of which made up her master's thesis at the University of Iowa - <i>slowly</i>, only a few a day. I took notes as I was going and read as much analysis as I could on each story. What an experience, to immerse myself in this author's life work.<br/><br/>It's a dark place to </span>
  <span id="freeText15117958360863813157" style="display:none">I feel like I've just been to school. (That's a good thing.) I read each of these 31 stories - a compilation of both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48464.A_Good_Man_is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories" title="A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218659.Everything_That_Rises_Must_Converge_Stories" title="Everything That Rises Must Converge Stories by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">Everything That Rises Must Converge: Stories</a>, as well as 12 other stories, 6 of which made up her master's thesis at the University of Iowa - <i>slowly</i>, only a few a day. I took notes as I was going and read as much analysis as I could on each story. What an experience, to immerse myself in this author's life work.<br /><br />It's a dark place to be, though I've always liked dark. Flannery O'Connor's literary world is beyond bleak, to the point where if one of her characters smiles, you notice with a breath of relief, ahhhh, a tiny respite from the hard lives and harder hearts on display here. The sky and the sun and of course peacocks get all sorts of glorious description in these stories. But the PEOPLE... the people are hopeless and selfish, grappling for control of their meagre lives on a slippery surface that affords no purchase.<br /><br />Flannery O'Connor's name goes hand in hand with "Southern gothic", though she used "Christian realism" to describe the toughness of her stories. In my opinion, both apply to her work. Most of her stories take place in bedraggled farms in the American South, with tough characters who often possess ironic names (Mrs. Cope can't cope, Sheppard can't lead anyone, Shiftlet is definitely shifty, Crater is a void, Pointer is a cruel phallus, etc). The lessons are told using allegory dotted with symbolism. After you've read a few of her stories, you will notice a pattern. Despite the dank darkness of the lives she adorns her characters with, there is always an opportunity for <b>grace</b>, the chance to choose right. If they do not choose correctly, woe betide them, for all sorts of terrible punishments are ahead, in the form of death and loss and isolation.<br /><br />Even though I recognised this pattern like a beacon, I couldn't help but sympathise and identify with the characters who were on their road to ruin. I mean, who <i>wouldn't</i> be annoyed if someone else's bull was loose in your farm, wrecking everything? That, I believe, is where much of O'Connor's power lies. The 'villains' in her stories are us, everyday people, who are snared in our humanity, our time, our weaknesses. It is we who struggle every day at achieving grace. And that is what pierces the heart of anyone who reads these stories.<br /><br />She addresses racism many, many times over - which sadly, still remains a timely issue. And she has a hard eye for 'intellectuals' - <i>none</i> of them know nearly as much as they think they know.<br /><br />The collection was a little uneven for me. <i>The Train</i>, <i>The Peeler</i> and any others featuring Hazel and Enoch did not interest me much. That probably means I should stay clear of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48467.Wise_Blood" title="Wise Blood by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">Wise Blood</a>, because these stories eventually became part of this novel. Also <i>You Can't Be Any Poorer than Dead</i> which eventually became part of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48468.The_Violent_Bear_It_Away" title="The Violent Bear It Away by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">The Violent Bear It Away</a>, and <i>Why Do the Heathen Rage?</i> which was meant to be part of a future novel - neither worked for me as short stories.<br /><br />However, there is so much gold here, it is easy to let go of what doesn't impress and stay with the sparkling jewels such as:<br /><br /><i>The Geranium</i> - an old Southern man's inability to adjust to life in NYC (later re-written as <i>Judgment Day</i>, her last story)<br /><br /><i>The Barber</i> - a fascinating image of "casting pearls to swine", showing the insecure need to change people's minds to match one's own, and the ineffectuality of intellectual arguments<br /><br /><i>A Good Man is Hard to Find</i> - her most famous story, when a family trip is savaged while making a stop to visit an old plantation property. Punishment for glorifying an imperfect past is doled out, for thinking in terms of "them" and "us". Begs the question, "what makes a person good"?<br /><br /><i>A Circle in the Fire</i> - a woman who runs a farm is visited by some boys, who torment her, instil fear and menace, and demonstrate that she is NOT in charge<br /><br /><i>The Displaced Person</i> - a story of tremendous power about a woman who takes in a Polish DP to work on her farm. His efficiency does not sit well with the rest of the farm, and what ensues in a sick, slow build up, made me gasp.<br /><br /><i>Greenleaf</i> - another woman on a farm (pretty much everyone in O'Connor's stories are widows or widowers, and there's almost always a red-headed person in each story) has to deal with an errant bull on her property, with deathly consequences<br /><br /><i>Everything that Rises Must Converge</i> - brilliant tale of moral ambiguity, taking place on an integrated bus ride<br /><br />Her disturbing, damning stories will linger in my mind. These stories continue to exert their power, a pointing finger, a morally all-seeing eye that cuts and exposes without mercy. Wow.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15117958360863813157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_978766911" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/978766911" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/978766911?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=anthology">anthology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=fiction-general">fiction-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer978766911" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11242656719548501042">In February 1948, Flannery O'Connor, a graduate of the MFA program at the University of Iowa, was twenty-three years old and eager to please the publishing industry with the beginning chapters of a novel-in-progress titled <i>Wise Blood</i>. A letter O'Connor received from one such publisher was not receptive. He commended her for being a straight-shooter and added that she was gifted, but with a loneliness in her work, as if she were writing simply out of her own experience. <br/><br/>O'Connor responded to a fr</span>
  <span id="freeText11242656719548501042" style="display:none">In February 1948, Flannery O'Connor, a graduate of the MFA program at the University of Iowa, was twenty-three years old and eager to please the publishing industry with the beginning chapters of a novel-in-progress titled <i>Wise Blood</i>. A letter O'Connor received from one such publisher was not receptive. He commended her for being a straight-shooter and added that she was gifted, but with a loneliness in her work, as if she were writing simply out of her own experience. <br /><br />O'Connor responded to a friend. "Please tell me what is behind this Sears Roebuck Straight Shooter approach. I presume ... either that [the publisher] will not take the novel as it will be left to my fiendish care (it will essentially be as it is) , or that [the publisher] would like to rescue it at this point and train it into a conventional novel ... The letter is addressed to a slightly dim-witted Campfire Girl, and I cannot look forward with composure to a lifetime of others like them."<br /><br />Unconventional in dazzling ways, I felt that O'Connor struggled a bit to sustain <i>Wise Blood</i> around one character. Her morbid wit, fascination with God's lonely man and fearless search for truth in a society coming apart with change are perfectly suited for the short story format. <i>The Complete Stories</i>, published posthumously in 1971, contains thirty-one tales, each more powerful and haunting than the last. As a sum of its parts, it's one of my favorite books.<br /><br />Four of the stories -- <i>The Train</i>, <i>The Peeler</i>, <i>The Heart of the Park</i> and <i>Enoch and the Gorilla</i> -- were revised by O'Connor and became chapters of <i>Wise Blood</i>. They're prelude to at least six stories that grabbed me and threw me across the room:<br /><br /><i>A Good Man Is Hard To Find</i> in which a grandmother's insistence on visiting a plantation from her youth, while on a road trip with her son, daughter-in-law and three grandchildren puts them on a collision course with an escaped fugitive dubbed The Misfit.<br /><br /><i>A Circle in the Fire</i> in which a nervous farm widow is visited by a teenaged boy who once lived on her land and returns with two friends from the city. The dangerous boys love the country so much that they refuse to leave without taking some of it with them.<br /><br /><i>The Displaced Person</i> in which a Polish refugee and his family are given the chance to start a new life in America working on a farm, but quietly plague the good country people with their work ethic, disquiet and alien ways.<br /><br /><i>Greenleaf</i> in which a proud farm widow, with two grown sons averse to manual labor, is bedeviled by the appearance of a stray bull on her property, a beast she determines belongs to the sons of her belligerent farm hand, Mr. Greenleaf.<br /><br /><i>Everything That Rises Must Converge</i> in which a progressive minded man disgusted by bigoted ways of his mother agrees to accompany her on an errand, using a desegregated night bus in an attempt to prove a point to the old bat. The lesson ends up becoming his.<br /><br /><i>The Lame Shall Enter First</i> in which a widowed recreational director who's given up hope his son will contribute anything positive to society offers room and board and a second chance to a juvenile delinquent with a 140 IQ and club foot, so full of potential the man can't resist saving him.<br /><br />O'Connor's characters have holes they're struggling to fill -- with education, progressive ideals, charity, Jesus Christ -- but they end up digging themselves even deeper holes. These are haunted people and several of these tales were eerie enough to keep me awake at night. O'Connor doesn't go for ghosts or goblins, but her characters are visited by their share of demons. <br /><br />The tension in O'Connor's storytelling is softened by her dark wit and powerful observation. Her character descriptions often set the table in a household Charles Addams would feel at home in:<br /><br /><i>"The doctors had told Mrs. Hopewell that with the best of care, Joy might see forty-five. She had a weak heart. Joy had made it plain that if it had not been for this condition, she would be far from these red hills and good country people. She would be in a university lecturing to people who knew what she was talking about. And Mrs. Hopewell could very well picture her there, looking like a scarecrow and lecturing to more of the same. Here she went about all day in a six-year-old skirt and a yellow sweat shirt with a faded cowboy on a horse embossed on it. She thought this was funny; Mrs. Hopewell thought it was idiotic and simply showed that she was still a child. She was brilliant but she didn't have a grain of sense. It seemed to Mrs. Hopewell that every year she grew less like other people and more like herself--bloated, rude and squint-eyed."</i><br /><br />A common element in O'Connor's fiction is the progressive grown child -- the "Meathead" whom Archie Bunker was heckling on <i>All In the Family</i> the year this collection was published -- attempting to separate himself or herself from the hypocrisy of the mother, loving, but clueless as to what she represents to her children. Part of the genius of these stories, apart from how taut they are with tension, is how O'Connor refuses to pass judgment on either side of the culture war. Liberals can believe O'Connor is attacking the good ole boy network, while the Archie Bunkers could actually view these stories as a rebuke of the Meatheads, coming from one of their own, a writer reared in Savannah, GA. I think the truth is a lot more complicated than either position and is explored beautifully in this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11242656719548501042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1333916832" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333916832" />
    <a title="booklady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/350218-booklady"><img alt="booklady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591723471p2/350218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1333916832?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="booklady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="booklady" href="/user/show/350218-booklady">booklady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2006">2006</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2008">2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=worth-reading-over-and-over">worth-reading-over-and-over</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333916832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16974219793571369105">In <u>The Geranium</u>, Old Dudley is the proverbial fish-out-of-water, overwhelmed by his environment, regretting his choice to trade familiar small town for a chance to see the Big Apple. To escape the constant onslaught on his senses, he’s fixated on the daily regimen of a neighbor’s geranium, the closest thing to nature, i.e., ‘back home’ he’s found. But in a twist comparable to the best of O’Henry, Dudley’s prejudice is revealed by unwelcome kindness from an ‘enemy’ and animosity comes to him from</span>
  <span id="freeText16974219793571369105" style="display:none">In <u>The Geranium</u>, Old Dudley is the proverbial fish-out-of-water, overwhelmed by his environment, regretting his choice to trade familiar small town for a chance to see the Big Apple. To escape the constant onslaught on his senses, he’s fixated on the daily regimen of a neighbor’s geranium, the closest thing to nature, i.e., ‘back home’ he’s found. But in a twist comparable to the best of O’Henry, Dudley’s prejudice is revealed by unwelcome kindness from an ‘enemy’ and animosity comes to him from an equally improbable source. A<br /><br />What is the saying? ‘A fool convinced against his will is of the same opinion still.’ <u>The Barber</u> is the early 20th Century version of why you shouldn’t bother entering into arguments on the Internet. Back then everything you ever needed or wanted to know could be learned at the Barber Shop. A frustrating but wise read. B+<br /><br />In <u>The Crop</u>, 44 year old Miss Willerton, spinster story-writer escapes the humdrum reality of her life—as many unhappy women do—by fantasizing herself <i>femme fatale</i>, leading lady, of her own imaginary romances. In this case we’re given a glimpse of her co-stars. Charming. A-<br /><br />Who is <u>The Turkey</u>? Is it Ruller or what Ruller finds? On the cusp of emerging manhood, Ruller experiments with rebelling against his parent’s (especially his mother’s) rules concerning the name of the LORD and how to address the Almighty. What difference does it make if there is no one else to hear or see? A tale of two shot courage; one shot you see and one you don’t. A+<br /><br />In <u>The Train</u>, 19 year old Hazel Wickers (ne: Motes/Weaver) journeys by train to Taulkinham. We are taken along with him wandering insecure and confused—with flashes of extreme certainty—finding what? We watch the world go by as if we were the ones on a train. A runaway ride of confusing thoughts. This is the first of the four stories which O’Connor later revised into her novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48467.Wise_Blood" title="Wise Blood by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">Wise Blood</a>. B<br /><br />In <u>The Peeler</u>, Hazel Motes is walking the streets of Taulkinham, where he meets Enoch Emery, Asa Shrike, who is blind and a girl, Sabbath, traveling with him. Although physically blind, Asa sees more than anyone else, discerns the truth and speaks to more effect than the other three main characters. <i>“You can’t run from Jesus. Jesus is a fact. If who you are a-looking for is Jesus, the sound of it will be in your voice.”</i> A<br /><br /><u>The Heart of the Park</u> continues <i>The Peeler</i> and is the third in the series involving (at least some of) the same characters. Enoch Emery had tried to latch on to Hazel (Weaver this time) in the last story and when Hazel goes looking for him hoping to find out where the blind man lives—so he can hear more about Jesus—Enoch capitalizes on the opportunity to ‘share with someone special’. The two young men are abominable to each other, yet in their near total ignorance, they are as much pitiable as they are abhorrent. A-<br /><br /><u>Enoch and the Gorilla</u> is the perfect conclusion to the stories about the misfit Enoch who is so out of step in the world he doesn’t even know how much he is despised by everyone. It seems like every once in a while Enoch ought to accidentally meet a nice person or <i>someone</i> who likes him. They can’t be all beastly... or can they? B+<br /><br />In <u>A Stroke of Good Fortune</u> Ruby is disgusted with her brother Rufus because after two years military service he hasn’t learned to be ‘somebody from somewhere’. She’s the only one from her family to have escaped their now defunct town of Pitman by marrying Bill Hill from Florida who sells Miracle Products. And yet after all that climbing, why can’t she even go up her own stairs? B+<br /><br /><u>A Good Man is Hard to Find</u> is probably the most perfect short story ever written and certainly O’Connor’s best, and best known. Dysfunctional family on a road trip ends up stranded in the middle of nowhere; they encounter their worst nightmare. The goodness in the men <i>and</i> the women—in all of us—is hard to find. Superb dialogue at the end between The Misfit and the Grandmother. A++<br /><br />In <u>A Late Encounter with the Enemy</u>, 62 year old Sally Poker Sash’s nightly prayer is that her 104 year old grandfather, ‘General’ Sash will live long enough to see her graduate from college, never mind that he doesn’t know what is what anymore. A battle on many fronts, this must be read up ‘til the last sentence. Another one where O’Connor gives us an inside view. A-<br /><br />One armed Mr. Shiftlet appears one day—full of compliments and trivia at Lucynell Crater’s place. The two share much banter but little real conversation and no trust. In <u>The Life You Save May Be Your Own</u>, the two main characters are so focused on protecting their own interests they don’t see how they are being scammed and taken in by each other. B+<br /><br />In <u>The River</u>, Childhood is personified as little Harry/Bevel. He is the plaything of thoughtless and foolish adults who use him for their own selfish ends. In this ‘day in the life’ of Harry he learns that he ‘counts’ – although the precise meaning of this is never explained and he doesn’t know what to do with the information. A heart-wrenching exposé. A+<br /><br />Mrs. Cope in <u>A Circle in the Fire</u> had no sympathy for anyone else’s troubles. There was always plenty to be thankful for, no matter what bad happens because “it doesn’t all come at once” and of course it didn’t happen to her. But that philosophy and her worst fear get put to the test when three juvenile delinquents show up at her farm one day and refuse to leave. B<br /><br /><u>The Displaced Person</u> should be the displaced person<i>s</i> and yet it also works in the singular. It is about an entire family of Polish immigrants exiled from their homeland due to <i> ‘what was happening every day in Europe where they had not advanced as in this country.’</i> The Guizacs arrival at Mrs. McIntyre’s farm upsets the delicate balance and pacing of work. Mr. Guizac’s enthusiasm and work ethic aren’t appreciated by all. How place and pace are finally found and resolved is the stuff of this, one of the longest and best, of O’Connor’s short stories. Superb! A+<br /><br /><u>A Temple of the Holy Ghost</u> refers to the definition of the person given in 1 Corinthians 6:19: “Do you not know that your bodies are temples of the Holy Spirit, who is in you, whom you have received from God?” The unnamed child in O’Connor’s story relishes this understanding of herself and experiences an opportunity to apply it to one of the least in the Kingdom. A<br /><br /><u>The Artificial Nigger</u> is an unfortunate title. How so? Well for starters it refers to a plaster lawn statue the characters happened upon in a wealthy neighborhood. So O’Connor is not using the pejorative ‘N’ word in any way critical of African Americans. Rather she is ridiculing the snobbish insensitive pride of wealthy whites who have too much money and no compassion or taste. So much for the title. The story itself concerns a grandfather and grandson, coming to the big city, setting an old misconception straight—well actually more than one—and in the process re-encountering the oldest sin in the world, that of our first parents. Powerful tale of forgiveness and redemption. A+ <br /><br />In <u>Good Country People</u> Joy doesn’t want to be either: “Joy” or “Good Country People”. Since she blames her mother for an accident which has left her with a handicap, she uses this as justification to adopt a sour attitude to life. Even more, she had her name legally changed to Hulga because it was the ugliest name she could think of. One day in a moment of poetic justice, Joy/Hulga gets a little of her own unpleasantness. B+<br /><br /><u>You Can’t Be Any Poorer Than Dead</u> means—of course—that you can. There is economic poverty and spiritual poverty, and not what Jesus meant when He was talking about being poor in spirit. Rather, being poor <b>of</b> spirit... Fourteen year old Francis Tarwater had one task to perform for his uncle who raised him and wanted to leave everything to him. If Francis couldn’t even do that one simple assignment, who was actually the poorer man? A-<br /><br />O’Connor likes to explore the themes of blind envy and a taut battle of the wills. She does this in a number of her stories including, <u>The Life You Save May Be Your Own</u>, <u>A View of the Woods</u>, and <u>Good Country People</u>, but she is at her best here in <u>Greenleaf</u>. The deluded Mrs. May sees herself as the victim of her own employee, Mr. Greenleaf, his family, her own sons, and even a bull which keeps wandering where it shouldn’t. Her determination to prove her point does in fact bring it home for her. A<br /><br />Old Man Fortune lives with his daughter, son-in-law, Pitts, and their children but his real joys are his one granddaughter, Mary Fortune and using her to get back at her parents especially her father. Mary is the only one in the family he respects because he sees himself in her—physically as well as temperamentally. In <u>A View of the Woods</u> Mr. Fortune decides on a business transaction with a view to irritate his son-in-law but doesn’t figure on its wider impact. A<br /><br />Asbury went to New York to ‘escape the slave’s atmosphere of home’ and returned broken, sick, dying. Whatever his doting mother offers to do for him or suggests he do is met with his usual cold, unreceptive reaction. Indeed, <u>The Enduring Chill</u>, as title is also the temperature the main character, Asbury, carries with him wherever he goes. So now the question becomes, how long can this ‘enduring’ last? A<br /><br /><u>The Comforts of Home</u> is neither comfortable nor homey. Thirty-five year old Thomas’s home has been invaded by someone his mother feels sorry for, obliged to ‘help’. Sarah Ham AKA “Star Drake”, a self-proclaimed nymphomaniac, multi-failed suicide, congenital liar and parolee has taken up residence. Things go from bad to worse, until ... A<br /><br /><u>Everything that Rises Must Converge</u> recounts an evening involving the painstaking departure and bus ride of an adult son, Julian, and mother. Julian is accompanying his mother to her Wednesday night “reducing class”. It’s a lifetime’s worth of small talk compressed into a few tense and unforgettable hours. A+<br /><br />It’s the annual Azalea Festival in the small town of Partridge and everyone’s caught up in the spirit of the occasion. In <u>The Partridge Festival</u>, Calhoun and Mary Elizabeth are two young people bucking popular opinion, ‘the system’ if you will. They don’t believe in all this nonsense, especially not the consensus that a recent murder was committed by a madman. Surely he must have been fed up as they are with all this flower foolishness. He must have had enough and just couldn’t take it anymore. So they set out to find and visit Singleton in prison. B+<br /><br />In <u>The Lame Shall Enter First</u>, fourteen year old Rufus Johnson was being raised by an abusive grandfather in a shack without water or electricity. His father was dead and his mother was in the state penitentiary. He was mean, had a club foot, ate out of the garbage, and believed passionately in Jesus, the devil and everything in the Bible. Sheppard, atheist, widower and father of ten year old Norton, volunteered at the reformatory as a counselor on week-ends. Sheppard had ‘taken on’ Rufus because he believed he was the boy’s savior. Rufus saw right through Sheppard but it took the man longer to realize this, and much more important things. A <br /><br />Mrs. Turpin’s self-satisfaction meets an angry girl, Mary Grace, in <u>Revelation</u>. Both are among the colorful characters inhabiting a doctor’s waiting room which seems to grow smaller as the personalities emerge larger. While we grow more alert to Mary Grace’s disgust with Mrs. Turpin, she is oblivious to it, until it manifests itself. Mary’s Grace, or ‘gift’ if you prefer is an eye-opening opportunity for Mrs. Turpin. A+ <br /><br /><u>Parker’s Back</u> is a play on the ambiguity created by the dual meaning of the word ‘back’. Initially it seems that it refers to some return of the central character, O. E. Parker. But very quickly we realize Parker has tattooed almost every inch of his body <i>except</i> his back. His inability to break free from, or admit to, his first real love for a woman, who also happens to be his indifferent wife, combined with a profound experience set up a catharsis for Parker which bring both meanings of the word together in a poignant ending. A+ <br /><br /><u>Judgement Day</u> is a reworking or refinement of O’Connor’s first piece in this collection, <u>The Geranium</u>. The names are different but again an elderly father has come to live with his adult daughter in New York. Although he bitterly regrets his decision, he is resigned to it until he discovers his daughter is planning to renege on her promise to have him buried back down in Georgia. We take up the story as he is planning his ‘escape’, learning past details through flashbacks. Excellent on its own, quite apart from <u>The Geranium</u>, taken together the stories form perfect book-ends to this splendid collection! A+<br /><br />Although her stories were inspired and immortal from the beginning, there is no doubt O’Connor improved as she got older.<br /><br />Updated for grammatical errors: October 26, 2017</span>
  <a data-text-id="16974219793571369105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_738693838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/738693838" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/738693838?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=500-wm">500-wm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2014">r-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer738693838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16596688602606630534"><blockquote>
  <i>"Listen here," he hissed, "I don't care if he's good or not. He ain't <b>right</b>!</i>
</blockquote>A Stroke of Good Fortune. The Life You Save May Be Your Own. The River. The Displaced Person. A View of the Woods. The Lame Shall Enter First. Two of these are contained within <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218659.Everything_That_Rises_Must_Converge' title='Everything That Rises Must Converge by Flannery O'Connor' rel='nofollow noopener'>Everything That Rises Must Converge</a>. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48464.A_Good_Man_is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories' title='A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories by Flannery O'Connor' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</a> has the other four. Neither one would have done as much good in my estimation as the works in toto. Key word <i>my</i>.<br/><br/>Flannery O'Connor was an author whose name seeped</span>
  <span id="freeText16596688602606630534" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"Listen here," he hissed, "I don't care if he's good or not. He ain't <b>right</b>!</i>
</blockquote>A Stroke of Good Fortune. The Life You Save May Be Your Own. The River. The Displaced Person. A View of the Woods. The Lame Shall Enter First. Two of these are contained within <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218659.Everything_That_Rises_Must_Converge" title="Everything That Rises Must Converge by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">Everything That Rises Must Converge</a>. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48464.A_Good_Man_is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories" title="A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories by Flannery O'Connor" rel="nofollow noopener">A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</a> has the other four. Neither one would have done as much good in my estimation as the works in toto. Key word <i>my</i>.<br /><br />Flannery O'Connor was an author whose name seeped into my bones until there was nothing left but to read her. One class assigned me the solo 'A Good Man is Hard to Find' and left me baffled. A television show favored for its artistic atrocity and psychological vivisection featured the former and more as a psychology professor, turned FBI consultant, read to a comatose girl, potential serial killer. Godwin's Law turned O'Connor's Law whenever short stories were the question, a probability instantaneously one if favorites were asked for. The final blow was the every so often descriptor of "Catholic zealot", a religion whose childhood indoctrination may have fed my enthusiasm for theology but did nothing faith-wise.<blockquote>
  <i>"You won't be the same again," the preacher said. "You'll count."</i>
</blockquote>I acquired this book with the personal penchant of Go Big or Go Home in mind, eyed it back whenever I felt it eyeing me, and began. Now at the end, older and wiser and a few Wiki articles smarter, I say that if O'Connor's character are grotesque, I know an awful lot of grotesque people. I say that the archaic definition of awe of dread, terror, is not nearly as archaic as some would believe and far more hope. I say that if I wanted to understand O'Connor, I would have to understand the South, and to do that I would have to understand Catholicism, and to do that I would have to devote my life to literature in a much more concentrated manner than I am want to seriously consider.<blockquote>
  <i>"The world was made for the dead. Think of all the dead there are.</i>
</blockquote>Fortunately for O'Connor, morality is an uncomfortable nitpick for and will be so for the rest of days. Unfortunately for O'Connor, I read her long after my phase of existential grasping had faded to musing embers and the chance of conversion was ripe for the rotting. Fortunately, I am all too well acquainted with the tightwire between "I am a <i>good</i> person," and "I see me when I'm sleeping, . I know when I'm awake," to the point of nauseated pain, enough to see what she seeks to show in other things beyond the scope of religion and belief. Unfortunately, I am neither in love enough with her particular disturbation to seek her out before the very far future has come my way, nor am I certain that my positive judgment of her work hinges but a little on the whiteness of my skin. Conflict, conflict. Whether good or ill for her, she will long be kept as a subject of contemplation.<blockquote>
  <i>She was sorry that the poor man had been chased out of Poland and run across Europe and had had to take up in a tenant shack in a strange country, but she had not been responsible for any of it...[he] had probably not had to struggle enough.</i>
</blockquote>There's something ugly but true in all of her works, a vein that would do well to acquire a name deeper than the common 'hypocrisy' when realization of such often demands the death of the realizer, if not more. All for the reader's benefit, of course, the implication of 'woe to those who refuse to heed' thrown in free with sardonic glee. Not horror, but Old Testament. Not raison d'être, but your godforsaken soul.<blockquote>
  <i>"Oh, I see," the stranger said. "It ain't the Day of Judgment for him you're worried about, it's the Day of Judgment for you."</i>
</blockquote>I may not be Catholic, but that is not an "anything but".<blockquote>
  <i>"...she might experience a painful realization and this would be the only thing of value he had to leave her.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="16596688602606630534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8589902608555487551">Strange may it seem but I’ve never read anything about <b>Flannery O'Connor</b> and I didn’t know what I should expect so the book was like a lightning strike.<br/>“She saw the streak as a vast swinging bridge extending upward from the earth through a field of living fire. Upon it a vast horde of souls were rumbling toward heaven. There were whole companies of whitetrash, clean for the first time in their lives, and bands of black niggers in white robes, and battalions of freaks and lunatics shouting and cl</span>
  <span id="freeText8589902608555487551" style="display:none">Strange may it seem but I’ve never read anything about <b>Flannery O'Connor</b> and I didn’t know what I should expect so the book was like a lightning strike.<br />“She saw the streak as a vast swinging bridge extending upward from the earth through a field of living fire. Upon it a vast horde of souls were rumbling toward heaven. There were whole companies of whitetrash, clean for the first time in their lives, and bands of black niggers in white robes, and battalions of freaks and lunatics shouting and clapping and leaping like frogs. And bringing up the end of the procession was a tribe of people whom she recognized at once as those who, like herself and Claud, had always had a little of everything and the God-given wit to use it right.”<br />The world is split in two parts…<br />There are those who try to use the others and there are those who are just being used… <br />“A body and a spirit,” he repeated. “The body, lady, is like a house: it don’t go anywhere; but the spirit, lady, is like a automobile: always on the move, always…”<br />The majority is swarming and conforming – they are the people of the crowd, the cattle of the herd. Meanness is their weapon and ignorance is their creed.<br />“‘Why listen, lady,’ he said with a grin of delight, ‘the monks of old slept in their coffins!’<br />‘They wasn’t as advanced as we are,’ the old woman said.”<br />The minority consists of dreamers – they want to change the world, they want to fight the system, they pretend that meanness is elsewhere. But they are clueless, they cut a ludicrous figure and whatever they do they fail.<br />“He didn’t like anything. He drove twenty miles every day to the university where he taught and twenty miles back every night, but he said he hated the twenty-mile drive and he hated the second-rate university and he hated the morons who attended it. He hated the country and he hated the life he lived; he hated living with his mother and his idiot brother and he hated hearing about the damn dairy and the damn help and the damn broken machinery. But in spite of all he said, he never made any move to leave.”<br />Majority is never right but majority ever wins.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer22058955" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5101910507517302775">Since I won't be reading this collection straight through, I figured I'd rate the first 15 stories that I have read. Except for one here or there in anthologies, this is my first time reading her short stories and I can't believe it took me this long to get to her. They are amazingly good. <br/><b>April 29, 2009</b><br/><br/>*<br/><br/><b>April 3, 2016</b><br/><br/>Now I can't believe it took me seven years to get back to this volume, except for recognizing that O'Connor's unflinching worldview isn't always a lure and, of course, the main excu</span>
  <span id="freeText5101910507517302775" style="display:none">Since I won't be reading this collection straight through, I figured I'd rate the first 15 stories that I have read. Except for one here or there in anthologies, this is my first time reading her short stories and I can't believe it took me this long to get to her. They are amazingly good. <br /><b>April 29, 2009</b><br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>April 3, 2016</b><br /><br />Now I can't believe it took me seven years to get back to this volume, except for recognizing that O'Connor's unflinching worldview isn't always a lure and, of course, the main excuse of other books clamoring for attention. I find it appropriate, even though it was unintentional, that both times I read it around Easter. <br /><br />This time I decided to read one per night of the last 16 stories until I finished. That worked well, giving me time to digest each, but not too much time in between that I didn't recognize similar tropes -- for example, colorful tree lines with colorless skies above them. It's impossible to miss, no matter how much time passes, the recurring themes -- Pride as the ultimate Destroyer; Saving Grace arriving from frightening, unexpected places.<br /><br />Whether you agree or disagree with O'Connor's worldview, there's no denying the power of her writing. Her craft is impeccable. Her vision is inexorable.</span>

  <span id="freeText2024308839674040260" style="display:none">I have just finished the book unfortunately with a lot of effort, many years ago I had already read some of these stories and I liked them very much.....<br />I don’t remember them being so gloomy, totally violent where men drown in their grief, totally enveloped in their circumstances of daily tragedies...<br />I have read many articles on Flannery O'Connor, and I understand and I see the question "God is for the violent too" but in a Catholic perspective, here everything is hopeless, confused....<br />the souls who speak in these stories remain until the end without the possibility of redemption, of mercy.... as if the condemnation of men and the judgment were placed and given before the return to the Creator....<br />I’m completely confused and I don’t think I’m prepared enough to understand what O'Connor wants to tell us with these characters.....
